<p>The <b>1936 Summer Olympics</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i>Olympische Sommerspiele 1936</i>), officially known as the <b>Games of the XI Olympiad</b>, was an international <a href="/wiki/Multi-sport_event" title="Multi-sport event">multi-sport event</a> that was held in 1936 in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Germany</a>. Berlin won the bid to host the Games over <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Spanish_Republic" title="Second Spanish Republic">Spain</a>, on 26 April 1931, at the 29th <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IOC_Session" title="IOC Session">IOC Session</a> in Barcelona (two years before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nazis" title="Nazis">Nazis</a> came to power). It marked the second and final time the <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a> gathered to vote in a city that was bidding to host those Games.</p>
<p>To outdo the <a href="/wiki/1932_Summer_Olympics" title="1932 Summer Olympics">Los Angeles games of 1932</a>, Adolf Hitler had built <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Olympiad_(Berlin)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Olympiad (Berlin) (page does not exist)">a new 100,000-seat track and field stadium</a>, six gymnasiums, and many other smaller arenas. The games were the first to be <a href="/wiki/Fernsehsender_Paul_Nipkow" title="Fernsehsender Paul Nipkow">televised</a>, and radio broadcasts reached 41 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-1">[1]</a></sup> Filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Leni_Riefenstahl" title="Leni Riefenstahl">Leni Riefenstahl</a> was commissioned by the German Olympic Committee to film the Games for $7 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-1">[1]</a></sup> Her film, titled <i><a href="/wiki/Olympia_(1938_film)" title="Olympia (1938 film)">Olympia</a></i>, pioneered many of the techniques now common in the filming of sports.</p>
<p>Reich Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> saw the Games as an opportunity to promote his government and ideals of racial supremacy, and the official Nazi party paper, the <i><a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkischer_Beobachter" title="Völkischer Beobachter">Völkischer Beobachter</a></i>, wrote in the strongest terms that Jews should not be allowed to participate in the Games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitlerland._p._188_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hitlerland._p._188-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Clay_Large_p._58_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-David_Clay_Large_p._58-3">[3]</a></sup> When threatened with a boycott of the Games by other nations, Hitler appeared to allow athletes of other ethnicities from other countries to participate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> However German Jewish athletes were barred or prevented from taking part by a variety of methods<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and Jewish athletes from other countries (notably the US) seem to have been side-lined in order not to offend the Nazi government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Total ticket revenues were 7.5 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Reichsmark" title="German Reichsmark">Reichsmark</a>, generating a profit of over one million marks. The official budget did not include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outlay" title="Outlay">outlays</a> by the city of Berlin (which issued an itemized report detailing its costs of 16.5 million marks) or outlays of the German national government (which did not make its costs public, but is estimated to have spent US$30 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zarnowski_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zarnowski-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jesse_Owens" title="Jesse Owens">Jesse Owens</a> won four gold medals in the sprint and <a href="/wiki/Long_jump" title="Long jump">long jump</a> events and became the most successful athlete to compete in Berlin while the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">host country</a> was the most successful country overall with 89 medals total, with the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> coming in second with 56 medals. These were the final Olympics under the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Henri_de_Baillet-Latour" title="Henri de Baillet-Latour">Henri de Baillet-Latour</a> and the final Olympic Games for 12 years because of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The next Olympic Games would be held in 1948 (the <a href="/wiki/1948_Winter_Olympics" title="1948 Winter Olympics">Winter</a> in Switzerland and then the <a href="/wiki/1948_Summer_Olympics" title="1948 Summer Olympics">Summer</a> in London).</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Host_city_selection">Host city selection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Host city selection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The bidding for these Olympic Games was the first to be contested by IOC members casting votes for their own favorite host cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The vote occurred in 1931, during the <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Weimar Republic</a>, before <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> rose to power in 1933. Many other cities around the world also wanted to host the Summer Olympics for that year, but except for <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a> they did not receive any IOC votes. The other cities competing to hold the games were <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lausanne" title="Lausanne">Lausanne</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg" title="Nuremberg">Nuremberg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Budapest" title="Budapest">Budapest</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-votes_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-votes-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The selection procedure marked the second and final time that the <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a> would gather to vote in a city which was bidding to host those Games. The only other time this occurred was at the inaugural IOC Session in Paris, France, on 24 April 1894. Then, <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and Paris were chosen to host the <a href="/wiki/1896_Summer_Olympics" title="1896 Summer Olympics">1896</a> and <a href="/wiki/1900_Summer_Olympics" title="1900 Summer Olympics">1900 Games</a>, respectively.</p>
<p>After the Nazis took control and began instituting anti-Semitic policies, the IOC held private discussions among its delegates about changing the decision to hold the Games in Berlin. However, Hitler's regime gave assurances that Jewish athletes would be allowed to compete on a German Olympic team. In September 1934, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Olympic_Committee" title="United States Olympic Committee">US Olympic committee</a> publicly accepted the invitation to go to the Berlin games, halting any further IOC attempts to quietly revise the decision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The next scheduled <a href="/wiki/1940_Summer_Olympics" title="1940 Summer Olympics">games in 1940</a> were awarded to Tokyo. The Japanese military even demanded that venues should be built from wood because metal was needed for its <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">wars</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympic_torch_relay" title="Olympic torch relay">Olympic torch relay</a> – itself pioneered as part of the 1936 Summer Games – was to fly the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_flame" title="Olympic flame">Olympic flame</a> from Berlin to Tokyo in a specially-designed <a href="/wiki/Messerschmitt_Me_261" title="Messerschmitt Me 261">long-range aircraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> In 1938 the Japanese rejected hosting the games because they saw the Olympics and its <a href="/wiki/Pacifism" title="Pacifism">pacifist values</a> as 'an effete form of European culture'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mega_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-mega-13">[13]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-116-08A,_Olympische_Spiele,_Fackell%C3%A4ufer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="556" data-file-width="789" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-116-08A%2C_Olympische_Spiele%2C_Fackell%C3%A4ufer.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-116-08A%2C_Olympische_Spiele%2C_Fackell%C3%A4ufer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-116-08A%2C_Olympische_Spiele%2C_Fackell%C3%A4ufer.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-116-08A%2C_Olympische_Spiele%2C_Fackell%C3%A4ufer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-116-08A%2C_Olympische_Spiele%2C_Fackell%C3%A4ufer.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-116-08A%2C_Olympische_Spiele%2C_Fackell%C3%A4ufer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1976-116-08A,_Olympische_Spiele,_Fackell%C3%A4ufer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Runners carrying the Olympic Flame</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hans_von_Tschammer_und_Osten" title="Hans von Tschammer und Osten">Hans von Tschammer und Osten</a>, as <i>Reichssportführer</i>, i.e. head of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalsozialistischer_Reichsbund_f%C3%BCr_Leibes%C3%BCbungen" title="Nationalsozialistischer Reichsbund für Leibesübungen">Deutscher Reichsbund für Leibesübungen</a> (DRL), the Reich Sports Office, played a major role in the structure and organisation of the Olympics. He promoted the idea that the use of sports would harden the German spirit and instill unity among German youth. At the same time he also believed that sports was a "way to weed out the weak, Jewish, and other undesirables."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Von Tschammer trusted the details of the organisation of the games to Theodor Lewald and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Diem" title="Carl Diem">Carl Diem</a>, the former president and secretary of the <i>Deutscher Reichsausschuss für Leibesübungen</i>, the forerunner of the Reich Sports Office. Among Diem's ideas for the Berlin Games was the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_flame" title="Olympic flame">Olympic torch relay</a> between <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and the host nation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Torch_relay">Torch relay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Torch relay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1936_Summer_Olympics_torch_relay" title="1936 Summer Olympics torch relay">1936 Summer Olympics torch relay</a></div>
<p>The 1936 Summer Olympics torch relay was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Olympic_torch_relays" title="List of Olympic torch relays">first of its kind</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> following on from the reintroduction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympic_Flame" title="Olympic Flame">Olympic Flame</a> at the <a href="/wiki/1928_Summer_Olympics" title="1928 Summer Olympics">1928 Games</a>. It pioneered the modern convention of moving the flame via a relay system from Greece to the Olympic venue. Leni Riefenstahl filmed the relay for the 1938 film <i>Olympia</i>.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The sportive, knightly battle awakens the best human characteristics. It doesn't separate, but unites the combatants in understanding and respect. It also helps to connect the countries in the spirit of peace. That's why the Olympic Flame should never die.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>, commenting on the 1936 Berlin Olympic Games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcasting">Broadcasting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Broadcasting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The games were the first to have <a href="/wiki/Live_television" title="Live television">live television</a> coverage. The German Post Office, using equipment from <a href="/wiki/Telefunken" title="Telefunken">Telefunken</a>, broadcast over 70 hours of coverage to special viewing rooms throughout Berlin and <a href="/wiki/Potsdam" title="Potsdam">Potsdam</a> and a few private TV sets, transmitting from the <a href="/wiki/Fernsehsender_Paul_Nipkow" title="Fernsehsender Paul Nipkow">Paul Nipkow TV Station</a>. They used three different types of TV cameras, so blackouts would occur when changing from one type to another. The quality was generally poor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Olympic_village">Olympic village</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Olympic village">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P019183,_Berlin,_Olympisches_Dorf.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="571" data-file-width="800" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P019183%2C_Berlin%2C_Olympisches_Dorf.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P019183%2C_Berlin%2C_Olympisches_Dorf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P019183%2C_Berlin%2C_Olympisches_Dorf.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P019183%2C_Berlin%2C_Olympisches_Dorf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P019183%2C_Berlin%2C_Olympisches_Dorf.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P019183%2C_Berlin%2C_Olympisches_Dorf.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P019183,_Berlin,_Olympisches_Dorf.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Olympic village</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 1936 Olympic village is located at Elstal in <a href="/wiki/Wustermark" title="Wustermark">Wustermark</a> (at <span class="nowrap"><span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;params=52_32_10.78_N_13_0_33.20_E_scale:8000"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">52°32′10.78″N</span> <span class="longitude">13°0′33.20″E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">52.5363278°N 13.0092222°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">52.5363278; 13.0092222</span></span></span></a></span></span>), on the western edge of Berlin. The site, which is 30 kilometres (19 mi) from the centre of the city, consisted of one and two-floor dormitories, a large dining hall, <i>Dining Hall of the Nations</i>, a swimming facility, gymnasium, track, and other training facilities. Its layout was designed and construction overseen by appointed village commander <i><a href="/wiki/Hauptmann" title="Hauptmann">Hauptmann</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_F%C3%BCrstner" title="Wolfgang Fürstner">Wolfgang Fürstner</a> beginning in 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Less than two months before the start of the Olympic Games, Fürstner was abruptly demoted to vice-commander, and replaced by <i><a href="/wiki/Oberstleutnant" title="Oberstleutnant">Oberstleutnant</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Werner_von_Gilsa" title="Werner von Gilsa">Werner von Gilsa</a>, commander of the <a href="/wiki/Infantry_Regiment_Gro%C3%9Fdeutschland" title="Infantry Regiment Großdeutschland">Berlin Guard-Regiment</a>. The official reason for the replacement was that Fürstner had not acted "with the necessary energy" to prevent damage to the site as 370,000 visitors passed through it between 1 May and 15 June. However, this was just a cover story to explain the sudden demotion of the half-Jewish officer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ripon_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ripon-19">[19]</a></sup> The 1935 <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Laws" title="Nuremberg Laws">Nuremberg Laws</a>, passed during the period Fürstner was overseeing the Olympic Village, had classified him as a Jew, and as such, the career officer was to be expelled from the <i><a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Two days after the conclusion of the Berlin Olympics, vice-commander Fürstner had been removed from active <i>Wehrmacht</i> duty,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and committed suicide because he realised he had no future under the Nazis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ripon_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ripon-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the completion of the Olympic Games, the village was repurposed for the <i><a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a></i> into the Olympic Döberitz Hospital (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Olympia-Lazarett Döberitz</i></span>), and Army Infantry School (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Heeres-Infanterieschule</i></span>), and was used as such through the <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a>. In 1945 it was taken over by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and became a military camp of the union occupation forces. Late 20th century efforts were made to restore parts of the former village, but little progress was made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> More recently, the vast majority of the land of the Olympic village has been managed by the DKB Foundation, with more success; efforts are being made to restore the site into a living museum. The dormitory building used by <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Owens" title="Jesse Owens">Jesse Owens</a>, Weissen House, has been fully restored, with the gymnasium and swimming hall partially restored. Seasonally, tours are given daily to small groups and students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The site remains relatively unknown even in Germany, but some tournaments are held at the site in an effort to boost knowledge of the venues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> An effort aimed at 2018 completion is underway to make the village a mixed residential and historical property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Venues">Venues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Venues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1936_Summer_Olympics_Reichssportfeld_map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2333" data-file-width="2303" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1936_Summer_Olympics_Reichssportfeld_map.jpg/200px-1936_Summer_Olympics_Reichssportfeld_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1936_Summer_Olympics_Reichssportfeld_map.jpg/300px-1936_Summer_Olympics_Reichssportfeld_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/1936_Summer_Olympics_Reichssportfeld_map.jpg/400px-1936_Summer_Olympics_Reichssportfeld_map.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1936_Summer_Olympics_Reichssportfeld_map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of Reichssportfeld during the 1936 Summer Olympics</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Twenty-two venues were used for the 1936 Summer Olympics.</p>
<p>Sailing was held in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Kiel" title="Bay of Kiel">Bay of Kiel</a>, which would serve as the <a href="/wiki/Sailing_at_the_1972_Summer_Olympics" title="Sailing at the 1972 Summer Olympics">sailing venue for the 1972 Summer Olympics</a> held in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The Olympic Stadium would later be part of two <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">FIFA World Cups</a> and then host an <a href="/wiki/IAAF_World_Championships_in_Athletics" title="IAAF World Championships in Athletics">IAAF World Championships in Athletics</a> along with undergoing a renovation in the early 2000s to give new life to the stadium. <a href="/wiki/AVUS" title="AVUS">Avus Motor Road</a> (AVUS) was started in 1907, but was not completed until 1921 due to <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kfi_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-kfi-27">[27]</a></sup> The track was rebuilt for the 1936 Games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kfi_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-kfi-27">[27]</a></sup> AVUS continued being used after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> though mainly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formula_2" title="Formula 2">Formula 2</a> racing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kfi_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-kfi-27">[27]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/German_Grand_Prix" title="German Grand Prix">German Grand Prix</a> was last held at the track in 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kfi_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-kfi-27">[27]</a></sup> Dismantling of the track first took place in 1968 to make way for a traffic crossing for touring cars that raced there until 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kfi_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-kfi-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>BSV 92 Field was first constructed in 1910 for use in football, handball, athletics, and tennis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The Reich Sports Field, which consisted of the Olympic Stadium, the Dietrich Ecekrt Open-Air Theatre, the Olympic Swimming Stadium, Mayfield, the Hockey Stadiums, the Tennis Courts, and the Haus des Deutschen Sports, was planned for the aborted <a href="/wiki/1916_Summer_Olympics" title="1916 Summer Olympics">1916 Summer Olympics</a>, but was not completed until 1934.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsf_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-rsf-29">[29]</a></sup> Mayfield was the last venue completed prior to the 1936 Games in April 1936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rsf_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-rsf-29">[29]</a></sup> Deutschland Hall was opened in 1935.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhb_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-mhb-30">[30]</a></sup> Mommenstadion opened in 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mstad_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-mstad-31">[31]</a></sup> Basketball was held outdoors at the request of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Basketball_Federation" title="International Basketball Federation">International Basketball Federation</a> (FIBA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wal1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-wal1-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The tennis courts were used, which turned to mud during heavy rain at the final.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wal1_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-wal1-32">[32]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Canoeing_at_the_1936_Summer_Olympics_%E2%80%93_Men%27s_K-1_1000_metres" title="Canoeing at the 1936 Summer Olympics – Men's K-1 1000 metres">K-1 1000 m</a> canoeing final was also affected by heavy rain at Grünau that included thunder and lightning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> During World War II, Deutschlanhalle suffered heavy aerial bombing damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhb_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-mhb-30">[30]</a></sup> After the second world war, the hall was reconstructed and expansion has continued as of 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhb_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-mhb-30">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Deutschlandhalle" title="Deutschlandhalle">Deutschlandhalle</a> in Berlin, where the boxing, weightlifting, and wrestling events took place, was used as venue, but was increasingly closed for repairs, last in 2009 when it was close for repairs, It was demolished in December 2011. the <a href="/wiki/Mommsenstadion" title="Mommsenstadion">Mommsenstadion</a> was renovated in 1987 and was still in use in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mstad_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-mstad-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Olympic Stadium was used as an underground <a href="/wiki/Bunker" title="Bunker">bunker</a> in World War II as the war went against <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>'s favor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The British reopened the Stadium in 1946 and parts of the stadium were rebuilt by the late 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> As a host venue for the <a href="/wiki/1974_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1974 FIFA World Cup">1974 FIFA World Cup</a>, the stadium had its roof partially covered on the North and South Stands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> British occupation of the stadium ended in 1994.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Restoration was approved in 1998 with a contractor being found to do the work in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> This restoration ran from 2000 to 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The modernized Stadium reopened in 2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> with a capacity of 74,228 people. The seating has been changed greatly, especially the sections that were reserved for German and international political leaders. The stadium now plays host to Hertha BSC (1963–present), and is expected to remain the home of the team for years to come. For the <a href="/wiki/2006_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2006 FIFA World Cup">2006 FIFA World Cup</a>, the venue was where the final took place between Italy and France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Three years later, the venue hosted the <a href="/wiki/2009_World_Championships_in_Athletics" title="2009 World Championships in Athletics">World Athletics Championships</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Games">Games</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Games">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opening_ceremony">Opening ceremony</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Opening ceremony">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P017045,_Berlin,_Er%C3%B6ffnung_der_XI._Olympischen_Spiele.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="560" data-file-width="800" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P017045%2C_Berlin%2C_Er%C3%B6ffnung_der_XI._Olympischen_Spiele.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P017045%2C_Berlin%2C_Er%C3%B6ffnung_der_XI._Olympischen_Spiele.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P017045%2C_Berlin%2C_Er%C3%B6ffnung_der_XI._Olympischen_Spiele.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P017045%2C_Berlin%2C_Er%C3%B6ffnung_der_XI._Olympischen_Spiele.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P017045%2C_Berlin%2C_Er%C3%B6ffnung_der_XI._Olympischen_Spiele.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P017045%2C_Berlin%2C_Er%C3%B6ffnung_der_XI._Olympischen_Spiele.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_B_145_Bild-P017045,_Berlin,_Er%C3%B6ffnung_der_XI._Olympischen_Spiele.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Parade of nations</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The opening ceremony was held at the <a href="/wiki/Olympiastadion_(Berlin)" title="Olympiastadion (Berlin)">Berlin Olympic Stadium</a>. A flyover by the German airship <i><a href="/wiki/LZ_129_Hindenburg" title="LZ 129 Hindenburg">Hindenburg</a></i> flying the Olympic flag behind it was featured early in the opening ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-59">[59]</a></sup> After the arrival of Hitler and his entourage, the parade of nations proceeded, each nation with its own unique costume. Some nations' athletes purposefully gave the Nazi salute as they passed Hitler. Others gave the Olympic salute (a similar one, given with the same arm), or a different gesture entirely, such as hats-over-hearts, as the United States and China did. All nations lowered their flags as they passed the Führer, save the United States. (The United States doing this was explained later as an army regulation.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-59">[59]</a></sup> Writer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wolfe" title="Thomas Wolfe">Thomas Wolfe</a>, who was there, described the opening as an "almost religious event, the crowd screaming, swaying in unison and begging for Hitler. There was something scary about it; his cult of personality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-owensdoc_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-owensdoc-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a speech by the president of the German Olympic Committee, the games were declared open by Adolf Hitler: "I proclaim open the Olympic Games of Berlin, celebrating the Eleventh Olympiad of the modern era."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-59">[59]</a></sup> Hitler opened the games from his own box, on top of others. Writer <a href="/wiki/David_Wallechinsky" title="David Wallechinsky">David Wallechinsky</a> has commented the event by saying that "This was his event, he wanted to be glorified."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-owensdoc_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-owensdoc-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_flame" title="Olympic flame">Olympic flame</a> was first introduced in the <a href="/wiki/1928_Summer_Olympics" title="1928 Summer Olympics">1928 Summer Olympics</a> in Amsterdam, this was the first instance of the torch relay. The Nazis invented the concept of the torch run from ancient <a href="/wiki/Olympia,_Greece" title="Olympia, Greece">Olympia</a> to the host city. Thus as swimmer <a href="/wiki/Iris_Cummings" title="Iris Cummings">Iris Cummings</a> Critchell later related, "once the athletes were all in place, the torch bearer ran in through the tunnel to go around the stadium". A young man chosen for this task ran up the steps all the way up to the top of the stadium there to light a cauldron which would start this eternal flame that would burn through the duration of the games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-owensdoc_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-owensdoc-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>But in spite of all the pomp and ceremony, and the glorification of Hitler, all did not go according to plan, and there was a rather humorous aspect in the opening ceremony. U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distance_runner" title="Distance runner">distance runner</a> <a href="/wiki/Louis_Zamperini" title="Louis Zamperini">Louis Zamperini</a>, one of the athletes present, related it on camera:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-owensdoc_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-owensdoc-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>They released 25,000 pigeons, the sky was clouded with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pigeon" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a>, the pigeons circles overhead, and then they shot a cannon, and they scared the <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">poop</a> out of the pigeons, and we had straw hats, flat straw hats, and you could heard the pitter-patter on our straw hats, but we felt sorry for the women, for they got it in their hair, but I mean there were a mass of droppings, and I say it was so funny…</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Events">Events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>129 events in 25 disciplines, comprising 19 sports, were part of the Olympic program in 1936. The number of events in each discipline is noted in parentheses.</p>

</div>
<p>Basketball and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Team_handball" title="Team handball">handball</a> made their debut at the Olympics, both as outdoor sports. Handball would not appear again on the program until the next German summer Olympic games in <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> in <a href="/wiki/1972_Summer_Olympics" title="1972 Summer Olympics">1972</a>. Demonstration sports were <a href="/wiki/Art_competitions_at_the_1936_Summer_Olympics" title="Art competitions at the 1936 Summer Olympics">Art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baseball_at_the_1936_Summer_Olympics" title="Baseball at the 1936 Summer Olympics">Baseball</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gliding_at_the_1936_Summer_Olympics" title="Gliding at the 1936 Summer Olympics">Gliding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wushu_(sport)" title="Wushu (sport)">Wushu</a></p>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BASA-3K-15-385-4-Volmari_Iso-Hollo,_1936_Summer_Olympics.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="917" data-file-width="625" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/BASA-3K-15-385-4-Volmari_Iso-Hollo%2C_1936_Summer_Olympics.jpeg/170px-BASA-3K-15-385-4-Volmari_Iso-Hollo%2C_1936_Summer_Olympics.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/BASA-3K-15-385-4-Volmari_Iso-Hollo%2C_1936_Summer_Olympics.jpeg/255px-BASA-3K-15-385-4-Volmari_Iso-Hollo%2C_1936_Summer_Olympics.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/BASA-3K-15-385-4-Volmari_Iso-Hollo%2C_1936_Summer_Olympics.jpeg/340px-BASA-3K-15-385-4-Volmari_Iso-Hollo%2C_1936_Summer_Olympics.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_achievements">Notable achievements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Notable achievements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Germany had a prosperous year in <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_at_the_1936_Summer_Olympics" title="Equestrian at the 1936 Summer Olympics">the equestrian events</a>, winning individual and team gold in all three disciplines, as well as individual silver in dressage. In the cycling match sprint finals, the German <a href="/wiki/Toni_Merkens" title="Toni Merkens">Toni Merkens</a> fouled <a href="/wiki/Arie_van_Vliet" title="Arie van Vliet">Arie van Vliet</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. Instead of being disqualified, he was fined 100 marks and kept his gold. German gymnasts <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Frey" title="Konrad Frey">Konrad Frey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Schwarzmann" title="Alfred Schwarzmann">Alfred Schwarzmann</a> both won three gold medals.</p>
<p>American <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Owens" title="Jesse Owens">Jesse Owens</a> won four gold medals in the sprint and <a href="/wiki/Long_jump" title="Long jump">long jump</a> events. His German competitor <a href="/wiki/Luz_Long" title="Luz Long">Luz Long</a> offered Owens advice after he almost failed to qualify in the long jump and was posthumously awarded the <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Coubertin_medal" title="Pierre de Coubertin medal">Pierre de Coubertin medal</a> for sportsmanship. <a href="/wiki/Mack_Robinson_(athlete)" title="Mack Robinson (athlete)">Mack Robinson</a>, brother to <a href="/wiki/Jackie_Robinson" title="Jackie Robinson">Jackie Robinson</a>, won the 200-meter sprint silver medal behind Owens by 0.4 seconds. Although he did not win a medal, future American war hero <a href="/wiki/Louis_Zamperini" title="Louis Zamperini">Louis Zamperini</a>, lagging behind in the 5,000-meter final, made up ground by clocking a 56-second final lap. This effort caught the attention of Adolf Hitler who personally commended Zamperini on his speed. In one of the most dramatic <a href="/wiki/800_metres" title="800 metres">800-meter</a> races in history, American <a href="/wiki/John_Woodruff" title="John Woodruff">John Woodruff</a> won gold after slowing to jogging speed in the middle of the final in order to free himself from being boxed in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Morris" title="Glenn Morris">Glenn Edgar Morris</a>, a farm boy from Colorado, won gold in the decathlon. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sport_rowing" title="Sport rowing">Rower</a> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Beresford" title="Jack Beresford">Jack Beresford</a> won his fifth Olympic medal in the sport, and his third gold medal. The U.S. eight-man rowing team from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">University of Washington</a> won the gold medal, coming from behind to defeat the Germans and Italians with Hitler in attendance.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_front.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_front.jpg/220px-WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_front.jpg/330px-WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_front.jpg/440px-WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_front.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Obverse of <a href="/wiki/John_Woodruff" title="John Woodruff">John Woodruff</a>'s gold medal for winning the <a href="/wiki/800_metres" title="800 metres">800 metres</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_back.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_back.jpg/220px-WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_back.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_back.jpg/330px-WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_back.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_back.jpg/440px-WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_back.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WoodRuff_1936_Olympics_medal_back.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Reverse of <a href="/wiki/John_Woodruff" title="John Woodruff">John Woodruff</a>'s gold medal while on display at <a href="/wiki/Hillman_Library" title="Hillman Library">Hillman Library</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pittsburgh" title="University of Pittsburgh">University of Pittsburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">PA</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jack_Lovelock" title="Jack Lovelock">Jack Lovelock</a> of New Zealand won the 1500 m gold medal, coming through a strong field to win in world record time of 3:47.8.</p>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marathon_(sport)" title="Marathon (sport)">marathon</a>, Japan won one gold (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Son_Kitei" title="Son Kitei">Son Kitei</a>) and one bronze (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nan_Shoryu" title="Nan Shoryu">Nan Shoryu</a>) medal; both the runners were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_people" title="Korean people">ethnic Koreans</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> won the gold medal in the <a href="/wiki/Field_hockey" title="Field hockey">field hockey</a> event once again (they won the gold in all Olympics from 1928 to 1956), defeating Germany 8–1 in the final. However, Indians were considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Aryans" title="Indo-Aryans">Indo-Aryans</a> by the Germans and there was no controversy regarding their victory. <a href="/wiki/Rie_Mastenbroek" title="Rie Mastenbroek">Rie Mastenbroek</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> won three gold medals and a silver in swimming. <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Kristjan_Palusalu" title="Kristjan Palusalu">Kristjan Palusalu</a> won gold medals in both Men's heavyweight <a href="/wiki/Wrestling" title="Wrestling">Wrestling</a> styles, marking the last time Estonia competed as an independent nation in the Olympics until 1992.</p>
<p>After winning the middleweight class, the Egyptian weightlifter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khadr_El_Touni" title="Khadr El Touni">Khadr El Touni</a> continued to compete for another 45 minutes, finally exceeding the total of the German silver medalist by 35 kg. The 20-year-old El Touni lifted a total of 387.5 kg crushing two German world champions, El Touni broke the then Olympic and world records, while the German lifted 352.5 kg. Furthermore, El Touni had lifted 15 kg more than the light-heavyweight gold medalist, a feat only El Touni has accomplished. El Touni's new world records stood for 13 years. Fascinated by El Touni's performance, Adolf Hitler rushed down to greet this human miracle. Prior to the competition, Hitler was said to have been sure that <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Ismayr" title="Rudolf Ismayr">Rudolf Ismayr</a> and Adolf Wagner would embarrass all other opponents. Hitler was so impressed by El Touni's domination in the middleweight class that he ordered a street named after him in Berlin olympic village.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1936_Olympics_book_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-1936_Olympics_book-63">[63]</a></sup> The Egyptian held the No. 1 position on the <a href="/wiki/International_Weightlifting_Federation" title="International Weightlifting Federation">IWF</a> list of history's 50 greatest weightlifters for 60 years, until the <a href="/wiki/1996_Summer_Olympics" title="1996 Summer Olympics">1996 Games</a> in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a> where <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Naim_S%C3%BCleymano%C4%9Flu" title="Naim Süleymanoğlu">Naim Süleymanoğlu</a> surpassed him to top the list.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Italy_national_football_team" title="Italy national football team">Italy's football team</a> continued their dominance under head coach <a href="/wiki/Vittorio_Pozzo" title="Vittorio Pozzo">Vittorio Pozzo</a>, winning the gold medal in these Olympics between their two consecutive <a href="/wiki/FIFA_World_Cup" title="FIFA World Cup">World Cup</a> victories (<a href="/wiki/1934_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1934 FIFA World Cup">1934</a> and <a href="/wiki/1938_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1938 FIFA World Cup">1938</a>). Much like the successes of German athletes, this triumph was claimed by supporters of <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>'s regime as a vindication of the superiority of the fascist system. Austria won the silver; a controversial win after Hitler called for a rematch of the quarterfinals match to discount <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>'s 4–2 win over <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>. The Peruvian national Olympic team refused to play the match again and withdrew from the games. In the quarter-finals of the <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> tournament, Peru beat Austria 4–2 in extra-time. Peru rallied from a two-goal deficit in the final 15 minutes of normal time. During extra-time, Peruvian fans allegedly ran onto the field and attacked an Austrian player. In the chaos, Peru scored twice and won, 4–2. However, Austria protested and the International Olympic Committee ordered a replay without any spectators. The Peruvian government refused and their entire Olympic squad left in protest as did Colombia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>A remarkable story from the track and field competition was the gold medal won by the US women's 4 × 100 m relay team. The German team were the heavy favourites, but dropped the baton at one hand-off. Of notable interest on the US team was Elizabeth "Betty" Robinson Schwartz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anb.org_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-anb.org-65">[65]</a></sup> She was the first woman ever awarded an Olympic gold medal for track and field, winning the women's 100 m event at the 1928 Summer Olympics in Amsterdam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anb.org_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-anb.org-65">[65]</a></sup> In 1931, Robinson was involved in a plane crash, and was severely injured. Her body was discovered in the wreckage and it was wrongly thought that she was dead. She was placed in the trunk of a car and taken to an undertaker, where it was discovered that she was not dead, but in a coma. She awoke from the coma seven months later, although it was another six months before she could get out of a wheelchair, and two years before she could walk normally again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Due to the length of her recovery, she had to miss participating in the 1932 Summer Olympics in Los Angeles, in her home country.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Participating_nations">Participating nations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Participating nations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A total of 49 nations attended the Berlin Olympics, up from 37 in <a href="/wiki/1932_Summer_Olympics" title="1932 Summer Olympics">1932</a>. Five nations made their first official Olympic appearance at these Games: <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>, Bolivia, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a> and Liechtenstein.</p>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1936_Summer_Olympics_countries.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/1936_Summer_Olympics_countries.png/240px-1936_Summer_Olympics_countries.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/1936_Summer_Olympics_countries.png/360px-1936_Summer_Olympics_countries.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/1936_Summer_Olympics_countries.png/480px-1936_Summer_Olympics_countries.png 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1936_Summer_Olympics_countries.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nations participating for the first time shown in blue.</div>
</div>

<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti"><img alt="Haiti" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1000" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Flag_of_Haiti_%28civil%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_Haiti_%28civil%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Flag_of_Haiti_%28civil%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_Haiti_%28civil%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Flag_of_Haiti_%28civil%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_Haiti_%28civil%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Haiti_at_the_Olympics" title="Haiti at the Olympics">Haiti</a> also took part in the Opening Ceremony, but its lone athlete (a weightlifter) withdrew from competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Reich Chancellor Adolf Hitler saw the Games as an opportunity to promote his government and ideals of racial supremacy. The official Nazi party paper, the <i><a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkischer_Beobachter" title="Völkischer Beobachter">Völkischer Beobachter</a></i>, wrote in the strongest terms that Jews and Black people should not be allowed to participate in the Games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitlerland._p._188_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hitlerland._p._188-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David_Clay_Large_p._58_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-David_Clay_Large_p._58-3">[3]</a></sup> However, when threatened with a boycott of the Games by other nations, he relented and allowed Black people and Jews to participate, and added one token participant to the German team—a German woman, <a href="/wiki/Helene_Mayer" title="Helene Mayer">Helene Mayer</a>, who had a Jewish father. At the same time, the party removed signs stating "Jews not wanted" and similar slogans from the city's main tourist attractions. In an attempt to "clean up" the host city, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Ministry_of_the_Interior_(Germany)" title="Federal Ministry of the Interior (Germany)">German Ministry of the Interior</a> authorized the chief of police to arrest all <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Romani</a> (Gypsies) and keep them in a "special camp," the <a href="/wiki/Berlin-Marzahn_concentration_camp" title="Berlin-Marzahn concentration camp">Berlin-Marzahn concentration camp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_aspects">Political aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Political aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States_Olympic_Committee" title="United States Olympic Committee">United States Olympic Committee</a> president <a href="/wiki/Avery_Brundage" title="Avery Brundage">Avery Brundage</a> became a main supporter of the Games being held in Germany, arguing that "politics has no place in sport", despite having initial doubts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-70">[70]</a></sup> Later Brundage requested that a system be established to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gender_verification_in_sports" title="Gender verification in sports">examine female athletes</a> for what <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine called "sex ambiguities" after observing the performance of Czechoslovak runner and jumper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zdenka_Koubkova" title="Zdenka Koubkova">Zdenka Koubkova</a> and English shotputter and javelin thrower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_Edith_Louise_Weston" title="Mary Edith Louise Weston">Mary Edith Louise Weston</a> (both individuals later had sex change surgery and legally changed their names to Zdenek Koubek and Mark Weston).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>French Olympians gave a <a href="/wiki/Roman_salute" title="Roman salute">Roman salute</a> at the opening ceremony: known as the <i>salut de Joinville</i> per the battalion <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bataillon_de_Joinville" title="fr:Bataillon de Joinville">fr:Bataillon de Joinville</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_symbols#Olympic_salute" title="Olympic symbols">Olympic salute</a> was part of the Olympic traditions since the 1924 games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> However, due to the different context this action was mistaken by the crowd for a support to fascism (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympic_salute" title="Olympic salute">Olympic salute</a> was discarded after 1946).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a> only attended the opening ceremony, an interesting <a href="/wiki/Vexillology" title="Vexillology">vexillological</a> fact was noticed: its <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Haiti" title="Flag of Haiti">flag</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Liechtenstein" title="Flag of Liechtenstein">flag of Liechtenstein</a> were coincidentally identical, and this was not discovered until then. The following year, a crown was added to Liechtenstein's to distinguish one flag from the other.</p>
<p>American sprinters <a href="/wiki/Sam_Stoller" title="Sam Stoller">Sam Stoller</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marty_Glickman" title="Marty Glickman">Marty Glickman</a>, the only two Jews on the U.S. Olympic team, were pulled from the 4 × 100 relay team on the day of the competition, leading to speculation that U.S. Olympic committee leader Brundage did not want to add to Hitler's embarrassment by having two Jews win gold medals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1937, Hollywood released the film <i><a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chan_at_the_Olympics" title="Charlie Chan at the Olympics">Charlie Chan at the Olympics</a></i>. The plot concerned members of the <a href="/wiki/Ordnungspolizei" title="Ordnungspolizei">Berlin police force</a> helping the Chinese detective apprehend a group of spies (of unnamed nationality) trying to steal a new aerial guidance system. Despite pertaining to the Berlin Olympics, actual Games' footage used by the filmmakers was edited to remove any Nazi symbols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Olympics, Jewish participation in German sports was further limited, and persecution of Jews started to become ever more lethal. The Olympic Games had provided a nine-month period of relative calmness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antisemitism">Antisemitism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Antisemitism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The German Olympic committee, in accordance with Nazi directives, virtually barred Germans who were Jewish or <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Roma</a> or had such an ancestry from participating in the Games (<a href="/wiki/Helene_Mayer" title="Helene Mayer">Helene Mayer</a> was the only German Jew to compete at the Berlin Games). This decision meant exclusion for many of the country's top athletes such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shotputter" title="Shotputter">shotputter</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discus_thrower" title="Discus thrower">discus thrower</a> <a href="/wiki/Lilli_Henoch" title="Lilli Henoch">Lilli Henoch</a>, who was a four-time world record holder and 10-time German national champion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google2_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-google2-77">[77]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Gretel_Bergmann" title="Gretel Bergmann">Gretel Bergmann</a> who was suspended from the German team just days after she set a record of 1.60 meters in the high jump.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Individual Jewish athletes from a number of countries chose to boycott the Berlin Olympics, including South African <a href="/wiki/Sid_Kiel" title="Sid Kiel">Sid Kiel</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and Americans <a href="/wiki/Milton_Green" title="Milton Green">Milton Green</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norman_Cahners" title="Norman Cahners">Norman Cahners</a>. In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/American_Jewish_Congress" title="American Jewish Congress">American Jewish Congress</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Labor_Committee" title="Jewish Labor Committee">Jewish Labor Committee</a> supported a boycott.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USHMM_boycott_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-USHMM_boycott-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boycott_debate">Boycott debate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Boycott debate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prior to and during the Games, there was considerable debate outside Germany over whether the competition should be allowed or discontinued. Berlin had been selected by the IOC as the host city in 1931, but after <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler%27s_rise_to_power" title="Adolf Hitler's rise to power">Adolf Hitler's rise to power</a> in 1933, observers in many countries began to question the morality of going ahead with an Olympic Games hosted by the Nazi regime. A number of brief campaigns to boycott or relocate the Games emerged in the United Kingdom, France, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USHMM_boycott_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-USHMM_boycott-81">[81]</a></sup> Exiled German political opponents of Hitler's regime also campaigned against the Berlin Olympics through pro-Communist newspapers such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Arbeiter-Illustrierte-Zeitung" title="Arbeiter-Illustrierte-Zeitung">Arbeiter-Illustrierte-Zeitung</a></i>.</p>
<p>The protests were ultimately unsuccessful; in 1935 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amateur_Athletic_Union_of_the_United_States" title="Amateur Athletic Union of the United States">Amateur Athletic Union of the United States</a> voted to compete in the Berlin Games and other countries followed suit. Forty-nine teams from around the world participated in the 1936 Games, the largest number of participating nations of any Olympics to that point. However, in Spain, an alternative was organised in the IOC's second choice for a host city, <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>, although it was cancelled owing to supervening events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USHMM_boycott_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-USHMM_boycott-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Spain</dt>
</dl>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Olympiad" title="People's Olympiad">People's Olympiad</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Spanish_Republic" title="Second Spanish Republic">Spanish government</a> led by the newly elected left-wing <a href="/wiki/Popular_Front_(Spain)" title="Popular Front (Spain)">Popular Front</a> boycotted the Games and organized the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Olympiad" title="People's Olympiad">People's Olympiad</a> as a parallel event in <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>. Some 6,000 athletes from 49 countries registered. However, the People's Olympiad was aborted because of the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> just one day before the event was due to start, just as thousands of athletes had begun to arrive.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Soviet Union</dt>
</dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> boycotted the 1936 Summer Olympics. Instead, through the auspices of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sport_International" title="Red Sport International">Red Sport International</a>, it had participated in a left-wing workers' alternative, the <a href="/wiki/Spartakiad" title="Spartakiad">Spartakiad</a>, since 1928. The USSR had intended to attend the People's Olympiad in Barcelona until it was cancelled and did attend the <a href="/wiki/1937_Workers%27_Summer_Olympiad" title="1937 Workers' Summer Olympiad">1937 Workers' Summer Olympiad</a> in Antwerp, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> (both of which were Spartakiad events).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Turkey</dt>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Halet_%C3%87ambel" title="Halet Çambel">Halet Çambel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suat_A%C5%9Fani" title="Suat Aşani">Suat Fetgeri Așani</a>, the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkish_women" title="Turkish women">Turkish</a> and Muslim women<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> athletes to participate in the Olympics (fencing), refused an offer by their guide to be formally introduced to <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>, saying they would not shake hands with him due to his approach to Jews, as stated by Ms. Çambel in a <i>Milliyet</i> newspaper interview in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>United States</dt>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brundage_at_Berlin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="595" data-file-width="420" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Brundage_at_Berlin.jpg/170px-Brundage_at_Berlin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Brundage_at_Berlin.jpg/255px-Brundage_at_Berlin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Brundage_at_Berlin.jpg/340px-Brundage_at_Berlin.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brundage_at_Berlin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Avery Brundage meeting mayor of Berlin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julius_Lippert_(politician)" title="Julius Lippert (politician)">Julius Lippert</a> and IOC German Secretary Theodor Lewald in 1936</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Traditionally the USA sent one of the largest teams to the Olympics, and there was a considerable debate over whether the United States should participate in the 1936 Games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USHMM_boycott_81-3"><a href="#cite_note-USHMM_boycott-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Those involved in the debate on whether to boycott the Olympics included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ernest_Lee_Jahncke" title="Ernest Lee Jahncke">Ernest Lee Jahncke</a>, Judge Jeremiah Mahoney, and future <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IOC_President" title="IOC President">IOC President</a> <a href="/wiki/Avery_Brundage" title="Avery Brundage">Avery Brundage</a>. Some within the United States considered requesting a boycott of the Games, as to participate in the festivity might be considered a sign of support for the Nazi regime and its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Semitic" title="Anti-Semitic">anti-Semitic</a> policies. However, others such as Brundage (see below) argued that the Olympic Games should not reflect political views, but rather should be strictly a contest of the greatest athletes.</p>
<p>Avery Brundage, then of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Olympic_Committee" title="United States Olympic Committee">United States Olympic Committee</a>, opposed the boycott, stating that Jewish athletes were being treated fairly and that the Games should continue. Brundage asserted that politics played no role in sports, and that they should never be entwined. Brundage also believed that there was a "Jewish-Communist conspiracy" that existed to keep the United States from competing in the Olympic Games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-70">[70]</a></sup> On the subject of Jewish discrimination, he stated, "The very foundation of the modern Olympic revival will be undermined if individual countries are allowed to restrict participation by reason of class, creed, or race."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USHMM_boycott_81-4"><a href="#cite_note-USHMM_boycott-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>During a fact-finding trip that Brundage went on to Germany in 1934 to ascertain whether German Jews were being treated fairly, Brundage found no discrimination when he interviewed Jews and his Nazi handlers translated for him, and Brundage commiserated with his hosts that he belonged to a sports club in Chicago that did not allow Jews entry, either.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike Brundage, Jeremiah Mahoney supported a boycott of the Games. Mahoney, the president of the <a href="/wiki/Amateur_Athletic_Union" title="Amateur Athletic Union">Amateur Athletic Union</a>, led newspaper editors and anti-Nazi groups to protest against American participation in the Berlin Olympics. He contested that racial discrimination was a violation of Olympic rules and that participation in the Games was tantamount to support for the Third Reich.</p>
<p>Most African-American newspapers supported participation in the Olympics. The Philadelphia <i>Tribune</i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Chicago_Defender" title="The Chicago Defender">Chicago Defender</a></i> both agreed that black victories would undermine Nazi views of Aryan supremacy and spark renewed African-American pride. American Jewish organizations, meanwhile, largely opposed the Olympics. The <a href="/wiki/American_Jewish_Congress" title="American Jewish Congress">American Jewish Congress</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Labor_Committee" title="Jewish Labor Committee">Jewish Labor Committee</a> staged rallies and supported the boycott of German goods to show their disdain for American participation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-70">[70]</a></sup> The JLC organized the World Labor Athletic Carnival, held on August 15 and 16 at New York’s Randall’s Island, to protest the holding of the 1936 Olympics in Nazi Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Eventually, Brundage won the debate, convincing the Amateur Athletic Union to close a vote in favor of sending an American team to the Berlin Olympics. Mahoney's efforts to incite a boycott of the Olympic games in the United States failed.</p>
<p>US President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt">Franklin Delano Roosevelt</a> and his administration did not become involved in the debate due to a tradition of allowing the US Olympic Committee to operate independently of government influence. However, several American diplomats including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_E._Dodd" title="William E. Dodd">William E. Dodd</a>, the American ambassador to Berlin, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Messersmith" title="George Messersmith">George Messersmith</a>, head of the US legation in Vienna, deplored the US Olympic Committee's decision to participate in the games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USHMM_boycott_81-5"><a href="#cite_note-USHMM_boycott-81">[81]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=1936_Summer_Olympics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-ReferenceA-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ReferenceA_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Rader, Benjamin G. "American Sports: From the Age of Folk Games to the Age of Televised Sports" --5th Ed.</span></li>

The Daily Star<span class="reference-accessdate">.
